Technical\nInformation Division\n1990\n\nFOREWORD\n\nNASTRAN\xc2\xae\n(NASA\nSTRUCTURAL\nANALYSIS)\nis\na\nlarge,\ncomprehensive,\nnonproprietary,\ngeneral\npurpose\nfinite\nelement\ncomputer\ncode for\nstructural\nanalysis\nwhich\nwas developed\nunder\nNASA sponsorship\nand became available\nto\nthe\npublic\nin\nlate\n1970.\nIt\ncan be obtained\nthrough\nCOSMIC__ (Computer\nSoftware\nManagement\nand Information\nCenter),\nAthens,\nGeorgia,\nand is widely\nused by NASA, other\ngovernment\nagencies,\nand industry.\nNASA currently\nprovides\ncontinuing\nmaintenance\nof NASTRAN through\nCOSMIC.\nBecause\nof the widespread\ninterest\nin NASTRAN, and finite\nelement\nmethods\nin\ngeneral,\nthe Eighteenth\nNASTRAN Users\'\nColloquium\nwas organized\nand held\nat\nThe Red Lion\nPortland\nCenter,\nPortland,\nOregon on April\n23-27,\n1990.\n(Papers\nfrom\nprevious\ncolloquia\nheld\nin\n1971,\n1972,\n1973,\n1975,\n1976,\n1977,\n1978,\n1979, 1980, 1982, 1983, 1984, 1985, 1986, 1987, 1988 and 1989 are 3029.)\nThe Eighteenth\nColloquium\n.provides\nsome comprehensive\ngeneral\npapers\non the application\nof finite\nelement\nmethods\nin engineering,\ncomparisons\nwith\nother\napproaches,\nunique\napplications,\npreand\npost-processing\nor\nauxiliary\nprograms,\nand\nnew methods\nof\nanalysis\nwith\nNASTRAN.\nIndividuals\nactively\nengaged\nin the use of _inite\nelements\nor NASTRAN\nwere\ninvited\nto prepare\npapers\nfor\npresentation\nat the its\n\ngraphical\ncapabilities.\nA nonproprietary,\npublic domain\ncode mnemonically\ntitled CPI (for\nCOSMIC/NASTRAN-PATRAN\nInterface)\nis designed\nto manipulate\na large number\nof\nfiles rapidly\nand efficiently\nbetween\nthe two parent\ncodes.\nIn addition\nto PATRAN\'s\nresults\nfile preparation,\nis prompted\n\nCPI also prepares\nPATRAN\'s\nfor\nnecessary\ninformation\n\nP/PLOT\ndata files for xy plotting.\nduring\nan\ninteractive\nsession.\n\nThe user\nCurrent\n\nimplementation\nsupports\nNASTRAN\'s\ndisplacement\napproach\nincluding\nthe following\nformats:\n(1) static analysis,\n(2) normal\nmodal\nanalysis,\n(3) direct transient\nresponse,\n(4) modal\ntransient\nresponse.\nA wide variety\nof data blocks\nare also supported.\ntrapping\nand ease\n\nis given\nof use.\n\nspecial\n\nconsideration.\n\nA sample\n\nsession\n\nwith\n\nCPI\n\nillustrates\n\nrigid\nand\nError\n\nits simplicity\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nOverview\nThe standard\ngateway\nthat interfaces\nCOSMIC/NASTRAN\'s\nanalysis\nresults\nto\nPATRAN\'s\npost-processing\nmakes\nuse of NASTRAN\'s\nFORTRAN-written\nresults\nfiles.\nThese\nfiles can be requested\nvia DMAP\nALTER\'s\nOUTPUT2\nstatement\nin NASTRAN\'s\nexecutive\ndata deck.\nThey contain\ndata in mixed ASCII and binary code format.\nHowever,\nthey can not be used as direct\ninput\nto PATRAN.\nSimilarly,\nPATRAN\nalso supports\ncommunications\nwitla external\ncodes via specially\nformatted\nresults\nfiles.\nFormat\nof l_hese\nfiles is predetermined\naccording\nto PATRAN\nand differs\nfor each data type.\nGenerally,\nthey can be categorized\ninto three groups\naccording\nto their formats:\n(1) nodal results files,\n(2) element\nresults\nfiles, and (3) beam results\nfiles.\nThe number\nof results\nfiles can be as\nmany as required.\nTherefore,\nin order to interface\nCOSMIC/NASTRAN\nto PATRAN\nfor\npost-processing\npurposes,\nan interface\nthat is capable\nof translating\nfrom NASTRAN\'s\nanalysis results\nto PATRAN-recognizable\nfiles is required.\nFig. 1 shows\ncode and file relationships\namong\nthe analysis\nand post-processing\nmodules.\nThe flow begins\nwith the input of NASTRAN\'s\nanalysis\ndata deck.\nDMAP\nALTER\nstatements\nmust be included\nin the executive\ncontrol\ndeck in order\nto obtain\na\nFORTRAN-written\nresults\nfile. Generally,\nthis file is assigned\nan extension\nof .PAT.\nThis\nNASTRAN\nresults\nfile is then translated\nby the COSMIC/NASTRAN-PATRAN\nInterface\n(CPI).\nOutput\nfrom\nCPI is a group\nof PATRAN-compatible\nresults\nfiles and/or\na\nP/PLOT-compatible\ndata\nfile.\nThese\nfiles\nare ready\nfor PATRAN\npost-processing.\n\n1 PATRAN\n\nis a trademark\n\nof PDA Engineering\n\n2O\n\nPATRAN also requires accessto\nmodel.\n\nThis\n\nfile can be obtained\n\na neutral\nfile\nvia the COSPAT\n\ncontaining\n2 translator,\n\ngeometrical\nproperties\nof the\nbut not by means of CPI.\n\nJO T OT\nFILE/\n\nl..OOTI\nINPUT\nNASTRAN\nDATA\n\nI COSMIC/NASTRAN\nANALYSIS\n\n/\n\nCOSMIC/NASTRAN\nPATRAN\n\nINTERFACE\n\nTRANSLATOR\n\nCOSPAT\nTRANSLATOR\n\n* .DIS\n\n:DEOMETRICALI\nATA\n\nFILE\n\nOSPAT.PAT\n\n* .ELF\n\n/\n\n*.ERG\n*.ELS\n\n[\n\n*.GPF\netc\n\nPATRAN\nPOST-PROCESSING\n\nFig. 1 Code\n\nand File Relationships\n\nProgram\n\nDescription\n\nCOSMIC/NASTRAN-PATRAN\nInterface\n(CPI) translates\nCOSMIC/NASTRAN\'s\nFORTRAN-written\nresults\nfiles to PATRAN\ncompatible\nresults\nfiles.\nThese\nresults\ncan be requested\nvia the OUTPUT2\ninstruction\nin the executive\ncontrol\ndeck.\nprovides\nthe operation\nin two modes:\nMode\n\n1 - Whole\n\nModel\n\nTranslation:\n\nCPI\n\ntranslates\n\nall\n\ndata\n\nblocks\n\ncontained\n\nfiles\nCPI\n\nin the\n\nNASTRAN\nresults\nfile\n(*.PAT)\nand\ncreates\nPATRAN\ncompatible\nresults\nfiles\ncorresponding\nto data blocks\nfound in the input data file.\nThe user is prompted\nfor the\nprefix\nname\nof an output\nfile.\nDifferent\nprefixes\nallow the user to distingmsh\nbetween\ngroups\nof output\nfiles when many results files are translated\nin a single execution.\nUp to 6\ncharacters\nper prefix\nis acceptable.\nCreated\noutput\nfiles and translated\ndata blocks\nare\nsummarized\non the screen during CPI\'s execution.\nMode 2 - XY-Plot\nData Preparation:\nCPI creates\ndata for the specified\nnode or element\nfrom a set of data blocks.\nCPI prompts\nthe user for necessary\ninformation,\ni.e. node\nnumber\nand component\nnumber\nfor nodal data, and searches\nthe input file for any data on\n2 COSPAT\nAthens,\n\nis a COSMIC/NASTRAN\n\nto PATRAN\n\ninterface,\n\nGeorgia.\n\n21\n\ndeveloped\n\nby COSMIC,\n\nUniversity\n\nof Georgia,\n\nthis node.\nEach\ndata set is written\nto PATRAN\'s\nP/PLOT\ncompatible\ndata file.\nCPI\nnames this file XYPLOT.DAT.\nWhen this command\nis selected,\nCPI automatically\nreviews\nthe data blocks available\nin the current\ninput file. The user is then prompted\nto enter the\nnumber\ncorresponding\nto the data block name shown above this prompt.\n\nlimited\n\nCurrently,\nCPI\nto, the following\n\nsupports\nNASTRAN\'s\nrigid formats:\n\ndisplacement\n\napproach\n\nincluding,\n\nbut\n\nnot\n\nNormal Modal Analysis\n(Rigid Format 3)\nDirect Transient\nResponse\n(Rigid Format 9)\nti / Static Analysis (Rigid Format 1)\nModal Transient\nResponse\n(Rigid Format 12)\nTable\n1 shows data blocks\nsupported\nby CPI.\nIt should be noted that CPI supports\nany rigid format\nas long as the data blocks\nlisted are encountered.\nRigid formats\nnamed\nabove are only a guide to the rigid formats\nmost frequently\ngiving rise to these data blocks.\nCPI recognizes\ndata blocks, not rigid formats.\nTable 2 shows elements\ncurrently\nsupported\nby CPI.\n\nTable\nData\n\n1. CPrs main menu initiates\na prompt\nscreen\nwhich\nlists the various\ndata blocks found in COSMIC/NASTRAN\'s\nbinary output\nfile. CPI then\ninvites the user to specify desired\ndata blocks\ncontaining\neither\nnodal or element\ndata that\nare to be written\nto XYPLOT.DAT,\nwhich is the input file for P/PLOT.\nThis file can\ncontain\none or more data sets depending\non how many times the user requests\nthat CPI\nwrite to this file. CPI writes only y-data to XYPLOT.DAT\nsince the user defines\nan initial\nx and delta-x when executing\nP/PLOT.\nA description\nof XYPLOT.DAT\'s\nformat\nis given\nbelow.\n(1) Nodal\n\nData\n\nThe\n\nBlocks\n\nfirst line written\n"YDATA,\n\nfor each\n\ndata\n\nSET:\n\niii;\n\nDATA\n\nblock\n\ncontains\n\nNODE\n\nthe default\n\nnnnn;\n\ntitle:\n\nCOMPONENT\n\nj\n\n-\n\n"\n\nand may be appended\nto allow for more descriptive\ntitles.\nThis appendage\nplus the default\ntitle must not exceed 80 characters.\nIf more than 80 characters\nare specified,\nthose beyond\ncolumn\n80 are truncated.\nThis allows the user a suffix of anywhere\nfrom 33 to 38 characters\ndepending\non the number\nof digits contained\nin the default\ntitle.\nEach\n\nline\n\nthereafter,\n\ncontains\nnodal\ndata\nfor\nrespectively,\nas requested\n(2) Element\n\ndefault\n\nData\n\nuntil\n\neither\n\ncomponents\nfrom CPI\'s\n\na ^Z (end\n\n1, 2, 3, 4,\nprompting.\n\nof file)\n5, or\n\nor another\n6, i.e.,\n\nX,\n\ntitle\nY,\n\nZ,\n\nis encountered,\nOx, Oy,\n\nor Oz,\n\nBlocks\n\nAs with nodal data blocks,\ntitle and takes the form:\n"YDATA,\n\nDATA\n\nthe\n\nSET:\n\nfirst line written\n\niii;\n\nELEMENT\n\nfor an element\n\nnnnn;\n\nCOLUMN\n\ndata\n\nblock\n\nj\n\n-\n\ncontains\n\na\n\n"\n\nIt may also be appended\nas described\nabove, but again, the default\ntitle plus the appendage\nmust not exceed\n80 characters\nin length\nor truncation\nof characters\nbeyond\ncolumn\n80\noccurs.\nAs with the nodal data case, a suffix of 33 to 38 characters\nis allowed.\nThe\navailable\nprompt.\n\nfor\n\nremaining\nplotting.\n\nlines\nThe\n\nSOFTWARE\n\nassociated\ndata type\n\nAND\n\nwith the\nis chosen\n\nHARDWARE\n\ncurrent\ndata\nset contain\nelement\ndata\nby entry\nof a column\nnumber\nat CPI\'s\n\nREQUIREMENTS\n\nCPI source\ncode was written\nin standard\nFORTRAN\n77; with\n\nvery\n\ni0.0.\n\nrapidly.\n\ntable\n\nof\n\nUAI\n\nCOSMIC\n\nseen,\n\nNASTRAN\n\ninformation\nthe\n\n8 x\n\nconverge\n\nand\n\nfor\n\nAs\n\nof\n\nand\n\n65C\n\nresults\n\nsize\n\nlittle\n\nresults\n\nMSC\n\nelement.\n\na mesh\n\nconverging\n\ncore.\n\nNASTRAN\n88,\n\nalong\n\ncontain\nerror\n\n5\n\nCOSMIC\n\nvery\nboth\n\ntwo\nthe\n\nresults\n\nare\n\nfor\n\nshear\n\nin\n\ncommercial\n\nobtained\n\nthe\n\nnot\nand\n\nRESULTS\n\nOF\n\nTESTING\n\nUSING\n\nTHE\n\nMSC\n\nELEMENT\n\nTEST\n\nLIBRARY\n\nAs mentioned\nearlier,\nthe mesh and aspect\nratio studies,\nwhile\na very useful\ntool in the evaluation\nof an element,\ndo not test all of the important\nvariables\nthat affect\naccuracy\nin a finite\nelement\nsolution.\nThe MSC element\ntest library\nmentioned\nabove represents\na rather\nexhaustive\nseries\nof tests\nthat include\nmany of the element\nrelated\nparameters\nwhich\naffect\nthe accuracy\nof a finite\nelement\nsolution.\nReference\n3 gives a detailed\ndescription\nof the\ntest problems\nalong with theoretical\nanswers\nand the results\nof the testing\non\nseveral\nMSC elements.\nThe reader\nshould\nconsult\nreference\n3 for a complete\ndescription\nof the various\nproblems\nin the test series.\nThe portion\nof this\nseries\nof element\ntests that relate\nto the QUAD4 element\nwas run by the\nauthors\non the QUAD4 elements\ncontained\nin COSMIC\n88, UAI 9.8+ (not version\nI0.0 as for the mesh and aspect\nratio study)\nand MSC 65C.\nAs the MSC does in\ntheir\nreport,\nthe results\nare presented\nin detail\nand also in a summary\nform\nin which\nthe element\nis given a letter\ngrade of A through\nF based\non the\nmagnitude\nof the error.\nTable 6 shows the summary\nresults\nfor the 15 tests\nin\nthe series\nranging\nfrom a simple\npatch\ntest to modelling\nof beams\n(using\nthe\nQUAD4\nelement\nthrough\nthe depth)\nand various\nplates\nand shells.\nThe meaning\nof the letter\ngrades\nis given at the bottom\nof the table.\nAs pointed\nout in\nreference\n3, a failing\ngrade for an element\nin one test is not a reason\nto\ndismiss\nthe element.\nFor one thing,\nthe test scores\nwould\nimprove\nwith mesh\nrefinement;\nthe mesh used in most of the problems\nwas quite coarse.\nOf\nimportance\nin this discussion\nis not the actual\ngrades\nlisted\nin table 6 but\nthe comparison\nof the COSMIC\ngrades\nwith those from the other\ntwo programs.\nAs seen in table 6, the COSMIC\nQUAD4 element\nis as good as, or better\nthan,\nthose\nof the commercial\nprograms.\nAlthough\nnot shown\nin table 6, the old\nQUAD2\nelement\n(included\nin reference\n3) has a D or F grade\nin 9 of the 15\nproblems.\nThis is the reason\nfor the longstanding\nneed for an improved\nshell\nelement\nand the QUAD4\nelement\nadded\nto COSMIC\nNASTRAN\nclearly\nfills that need.\nDetailed\nresults\nfor each of the problems\nin the test series\nare contained\nin\ntables\n7\n12 and are included\nfor completeness.\n\nCONCLUSIONS\n\nThe COSMIC\nQUAD4\ngeneral\npurpose\nflat shell element\nhas been shown\nto be an\nexcellent\nelement\nand significantly\nenhances\nthe usefulness\nof COSMIC\nNASTRAN.\nThe element\nhas been shown to compare\nexcellently\nwith those available\nin two\ncommercial\nversions\nof NASTRAN\nthat are currently\nbeing used at the GSFC.\nThe\naddition\nof an improved\ntriangular\nshell element,\nanticipated\nin the near\nfuture,\nis highly\ndesireable\nas a companion\nelement\nto the QUAD4\nin general\nanalyses\nof complicated\nshell like structures.\n\n34\n\nREFERENCES\n\ni.\n\nCase,\nSixth\n\n2.\n\nCase, W. coupled-mass\nmatrix\ntends\nfor\nthese\nmodes.\n\nharmonics.\nto\nfollow\n\nThe\nthe\n\nSPAR,\nQUAD4\n\nExcept\nfor\na\nfew\ncases,\nthe\nfrequencies\nwere\noverpredicted\nfor\nconsistent-,\nlumped-,\nand\ncoupled-mass\nmatrices\nfor\nthe\ncylindrical-shell\nproblem.\nThe\nexceptions\nwere\nthe\nQUAD4\nlumpedmass\nmatrix\nand\nthe\nE43\ncoupled-mass\nmatrix\nfor\nthe\nfinest\nmesh\nfor\nthe\nsecond\nand\nthird\nmodes\nconsidered\nhere.\nFrequency\nis not\nthe\nonly\nparameter\nthat\nshould\nbe considered\nfor\nmodal\nanalyses.\nThe\nother\nis,\nof course,\nthe\nmode\nshape.\nOne\nmethod\nof\ncomparing\nmode\nshapes\nis\nto\ncompare\ncalculated\ngeneralized\nmasses\nfor\nthe\nsolutions\nusing\nthe\ndifferent\nelements\nbeing\nconsidered.\nAnother\nis\nto\nuse\na\nparameter\nfrequently\ncalculated\nwhen\ncomparing\ncalculated\nmode\nshapes\nwith\nexperimentally\nmeasured\nmode\nshapes.\nThis\nparameter\nis the\ncorrelation.\nBoth\nthese\n\npoints\nbeing\ncompared\nmeasures\nwere\nused\n\nin\n\nfrom\na straight-line\ncomparing\nsolutions\n\nfor\nthe\nn=8,\nm=5\nmode\nfor\nthe\ncylinder\nbeing\nconsidered\nhere.\nResults\nof these\ncomparisons\nare\nsummarized\nin table\nII,\nalong\nwith\ncomparisons\nof\nthe\npredicted\nfrequencies.\nThe\npredicted\nfrequencies\nare\nnormalized\nusing\nthe\nBOSOR\ncode\nresults\nas\nthe\nbaseline.\nThe\ngeneralized\nmasses\nwere\nnormalized\nusing\nthe\ntheoretical\nvalue\nobtained\nby direct\nintegration\nof\nthe\nsquare\nof\nthe\nanalytically\nperfect\nmode\nshape\nmultiplied\nby\nthe\nmaterial\ndensity.\nFor\nthe\nMSCC\ncomparisons,\nmode\nshapes\npredicted\nby BOSOR\nwere\nused\nas the\n"correct"\nshape.\nA study\nof the\nresults\nsummarized\nin table\nII shows\nagain\nthat\nthe\nlumped-mass\nmatrix\nprovides\nbetter\nfrequency\npredictions\nthan\ndoes\nthe\nconsistent-mass\nmatrix\nfor\nthe\nNASTRAN\nQUAD4\nshell\nelement.\nNote\nthat\nfor\nthe\n9-node\nby\n9-node\nmesh,\nthe\nerror\nfor\nthe\nconsistent-mass\nmatrix\nis over\n30 per\ncent.\nA finer\nmesh\n(17\nby\n17)\nwith\napproximations,\nlumped-mass\nin considerable\nmass\nmatrix\n\nshape\nresults\n\nthe\n\nconsistent-mass\nmatrix\nprovides\nbetter\nfrequency\nbut\nthe\nprediction\nis still\nnot\nas good\nas\nfor\nthe\nmatrix\nwith\na coarser\nmesh.\nThe\ngeneralized\nmass\nis\nerror\nfor both\nQUAD4\ncases\nin which\nthe\nconsistentis used.\n\nThe\ngeneralized\nerror\nthan\nthat\nused\n\nthe\nthe\n\nmass\nis a much\nmore\nsensitive\nMSCC,\nas\nevidenced\nby\ndata\n$8R5\nelement.\nHere\nthe\nMSCC\n\n65\n\nmeasure\nof mode\nfor\nthe\nABAQUS\nis quite\nclose\n\nto 1.0 for the 9-node by 9-node mesh, but the generalized\nmass is\nover 30 per cent in error.\nAs the mesh is refined,\nthe generalized\nmass improves,\nbut it is still\nnot as accurate\nas for the QUAD4\nwhen a lumped-mass matrix\nis used.\nNote that the performance\nof\nthe ABAQUSS4R5 element compares favorably\nwith the NASTRANQUAD4\nelement.\nThe SPAR E43 element, which performed\nnearly\nas well as the\nQUAD4 in predicting\nfrequencies\nfor all the shell modes considered\nin\nthis\nstudy,\napparently\npredicts\nboth\nthe\nfrequency\nand\ngeneralized\nmass accurately\nif the coupled-mass\nmatrix\nis used.\nHowever, somewhat unexpectedly,\nthis element does not perform quite\nso well with a lumped-mass matrix.\nIn this case, the frequency\nis\npredicted\naccurately\nbut the mode shape has considerable\nerror\nassociated\nwith it,\nas evidenced\nby the underprediction\nof the\ngeneralized\nmass.\nCONCLUSIONS\nAmong the elements considered\nin this study, the NASTRANQUAD4\nelement with a lumped-mass matrix seems to be the best choice when\nthe geometry is a cylindrical\nshell.\nA general\nrule\nseems\nto\nbe\nthat,\nshould\nfor\nthe\n\nfor\nbe\n\nany\nelement\navoided\nfor\n\nflat-plate\nlumped-mass\nThese\n\nmodeling\nconsidered\nderiving\n\nconsidered\nthis\nparticular\n\ngeometries,\nmatrix.\nconclusions\n\ngeometries\nhere.\nthe\nmass\n\nthat\nIt\nis\nmatrix,\n\nthe\n\nimply\n\nhere,\nconsistent-mass\ngeometry.\nOn the\n\nconsistent-mass\n\nthat\n\ndeviate\npossible\nsuch\nas\n\nchoices\n\nmatrix\n\nare\n\nmatrices\nother\nhand,\noutperforms\n\ndifficult\n\nfrom\nthe\nsimple\nthat\nan\nalternate\nthe\nSPAR\ncoupled-mass\n\nwhen\n\ngeometries\nmethod\nof\nmatrix,\n\nwould\ngenerate\na result\nthat\nwould\nbe\nmore\ngenerally\napplicable.\nNote\nthat\nit seems\nto perform\nwell\nfor\nboth\ngeometries.\nHowever,\nfor\nthe\npresent,\nif the\ngeometry\nis predominantly\ncylindrical,\nthe\nlumped-mass\nmatrix\nshould\nalways\nbe\nused\nwith\nthe\nNASTRAN\nQUAD4\nelement.\n\n66\n\nREFERENCES\n\ni\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\ne\n\nMarcus,\nM.\nS.;\nEverstine,\nG. FORTRAN\n77.\nThe\nboundary.\nand\neven\ndefinitely\ncomputers,\n\nadvantage\nof\nthe\nUNIX\noperating\nsystem\nis that\nit has\nno machine\nUNIX\nis becoming\nwidely\nused\nin many workstations,\nmini\'s,\nsuper-PC\'s,\nsome\nmain-frame\nccmputers.\nNASTRAN\nfor\nthe\nUNIX\noperating\nsystem\nis\nthe way to go in the future,\nand makes\nNASTRAN\navailable\nto a host of\nbig and ULTRIX\nworkstations.\nPreviously\nin 1988, an\n88 NASTRAN\nversion\nwas tested\nsuccessfully\non a SiliconGraphics\nworkstation.\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nThe\nadvantage\nof\nAT&T\'s\nUNIX\noperating\nsystem\nis that\nit\nis\nan\n"open\nsystem",\nhardware\nindependent,\nsingle\nand multiuser\nsystem,\npowerful,\nversatile,\nand reliable.\nThis\n"open system",\nwhich\nmay appear\nunder\ndifferent\nnames\nsuch as\nULTRIX,\nXENIX,\nSthn(_, AIX etc.,\nis becoming\nthe standard\nsoftware\ntoday\nfor the\nfast-growing\nmarket\nof workstation\ncomputers.\nEven IHM is going\nto adopt UNIX\nfor\nits forthcoming\nworkstations.\nAs many more computers\nare designed\nto run under the\nUNIX banner,\nthese\nnewcomers\nare getting\ncheaper,\nfaster,\nand more\npowe/ful.\nTne\n\n73\n\nresult:\n\nunprecedented price competition that\'s\n\ncomputing.\nway to go.\n\nThe\n\nmigration\n\nof\n\nTHE\n\nNASTRAN\n\nEARLY\n\nto\n\nthe\n\nmaking UNIX another word for\n\nUNIX\n\n"open\n\nsystem",\n\nis\n\ndefinitely\n\ncheap\nthe\n\nDEVELOPMENT\n\nNASTRAN\nis written\nmainly\nin FORIRAN\nlanguage.\nOnly\nabout\nfive percent\nof\nthe scurce\ncodes\nare machine-dependent.\nThe early\nstage\nof migration,\nstarted\nin\n1984-1985,\nwas\na move\ntowards\nANSI\nFOR_AN\n77,\nwhich\nis a standard\nFORIRAN\ncompiler\nfor all UNIX\nbased\ncomputers.\nIn this early\nstage\nof development,\nthe\nNASTRAN\nUNIVAC\nversion\nwas moved\nfrom SPACE\n\nVIBRATION\n\nFLIGHT HARDWARE\n\nANALYSIS\nUSING NASTRAN\n\nS. K. Thampi and S. N. Vidyasagar\nGE Government Services\n1050 Bay Area Blvd.\nHouston, TX 77058\n\nDuring liftoff and ascent flight phases, the Space Transportation\nSystem (STS)\nand payloads are exposed to the random acoustic environment produced by\nengine exhaust plumes and aerodynamic\ndisturbances.\nThe analysis of\npayloads for randomly fluctuating loads is usually carried out using the Miles\'\nrelationship.\nThis approximation\ntechnique computes an equivalent load factor\nas a function of the natural frequency of the structure, the power spectral density\nof the excitation, and the magnification\nfactor at resonance.\nDue to the\nassumptions\ninherent in Miles\' equation, random load factors are often overestimated by this approach.\nIn such cases, the estimates can be refined using\nalternate techniques such as time domain simulations or frequency domain\nspectral analysis.\nThis paper describes the use of NASTRAN to compute more\nrealistic random load factors through spectral analysis.\nThe procedure is\nillustrated using Spacelab Life Sciences (SLS-1) payloads and certain unique\nfeatures of this problem are described. The solutions are compared with Miles\'\nresults in order to establish trends at over or under prediction.\n\n9O\n\nINTRODUCTION\nDuring the past decade, the U.S. Spacelab program has made significant\ncontributions to the advancement of space exploration and research. The\nSpacelab is a reusable laboratory that is carried in the cargo bay of the Space\nShuttle. Experiments in several different disciplines such as astronomy, life\nsciences, and material science are accommodated in this modular laboratory\nfor various shuttle missions. The module also contains utilities, computers, and\nwork areas to support the experiments. The experiment hardware is mounted in\ninstrument racks located on either side of the module, in overhead lockers, or in\nthe center aisle, as shown in Figure 1.\nDuring liftoff and ascent flight events, the Shuttle and its payload are exposed to\nthe acoustic environment produced by engine exhaust plumes and\naerodynamic disturbances. Random vibrations are created by the response of\nthe module shell to the acoustic noise inside the cargo bay. The vibrations of\nthe shell are transmitted through the support structures (racks, mounting frames,\netc.) to the payload equipment. The vibration levels that the equipment has to\nwithstand depend on its own dynamic characteristics and its location inside the\nSpacelab. The equipment and its structural interfaces must be analyzed for\nthese random loads in order to ensure the integrity and flight worthiness of the\nsystem.\nThe analysis of flight hardware for random loads often relies on approximate\nformulations like the Miles\' relation (ref. 1) to generate limit load factors for\nstructural design. Due to the assumptions inherent in Miles\' equation, the\nrandom vibration criteria developed through this approach tend to be overconservative. In such cases, the results can be refined using alternate analysis\ntechniques like time domain simulations or frequency domain spectral analysis.\nThis paper describes the use of NASTRAN to perform spectral analysis to\nestablish more realistic design loads. The procedure is illustrated using the\nNeck Chamber Pressure System (NCPS) assembly which will be flown on the\nSpacelab Life Sciences (SLS-1) mission.\nANALYSIS BASED ON MILES\' EQUATION\nFor a lightly damped single-degree-of-freedom (SDOF) oscillator subjected to\nrandom excitation through its base motion, Miles\' relation is used as follows.\nNr=\n\n3 _/l-I\n2\n\nfn\n\nA\n\nQ\n\n(1)\n\nNr is the limit random load factor (g units), fn is the resonance frequency (Hz) of\nthe SDOF system, A is the power spectral density (g2/Hz)of base acceleration at\nthe resonance frequency, and Q is the dynamic magnification factor at\n\n91\n\nresonance.\nacceleration\noccurrence,\n\nThe quantity\nunder the square root represents\nthe mean square\nresponse\nand the factor 3 indicates\nthe 3-sigma\nprobability\nof\ni.e., the probability\nof exceeding\nNr is 0.26%.\n\nThe mean square\ncurve and is given\n\nresponse\nby\n\nis the area\n\nunder\n\nthe\n\n<u2> = j" S((o)IH((o)l\n\nwhere\n\nH((o) is the transfer\n\nfunction\n\nresponse\n\nspectral\n\ndensity\n\n2 d(o\n\nof the system,\n\n(2)\n\nS ((o) is the base\n\nacceleration\n\nspectral\ndensity\nfunction,\nand (o is the frequency\nin radians.\nThe derivation\nof\nMiles\' relation\nis based on the following\nsimplifying\nassumptions\nfor evaluating\nthe integral\nin Eqn (2).\n1) The\nfunction\n\nactual spectral density of base excitation,\nS (co), is a slowly varying\nin the vicinity\nof resonance.\nIt can be conveniently\napproximated\nby a\n\nconstant\n\nor white-noise\n\nsquare\n\nspectral\n\ndensity,\n\nA = S (COn), for computing\n\nthe mean\n\nresponse.\n\n2) Only the excitation\nenergy contained\nwithin the system\'s\nbandwidth\nis\ntransmitted.\nThe rest is filtered away by the system and does not contribute\nthe\n\nmean\n\nThese\n\nsquare\n\nassumptions\n\ndamping\npeaked\n\nfactor\n\nto\n\nresponse.\nare valid\n\nin the case\n\n_ << 1. For such\n\nat co = (On, and reduces\n\nsystems,\n\nof lightly\n\ndamped\n\nthe function\n\nto half its peak\n\nvalue\n\nsystems\n\nIH((o)[ 2 is very\nat a short\n\nwith\nsharply\n\ndistance,\n\n2F,(on,\n\non either side of the peak.\nThis distance,\ncalled the half power bandwidth,\nis\nvery narrow for lightly damped\nsystems.\nWith the assumptions\nmentioned\nabove, the integral\nin Eqn. 2 can be approximated\nby a rectangular\narea with\nbase equal to the bandwidth\nand height equal to the product\nof the constant\nvalue of excitation\nspectral\ndensity and the peak value of transmittancy.\nThis\ngives\n\n<u2>\nfrom\n\nwhich\n\nMiles\'\n\nrelation\n\n"\n\nI-[\n_- fnAQ\n\n(3)\n\nfollows.\n\nIn order to use Miles\' relation for the analysis\nof flight hardware,\nthe natural\nfrequency\nof the equipment\nis first determined\nthrough\nanalysis\nor test. The\ninput random\nexcitation\nspectrum\nfor the equipment\nis then determined\nas a\nfunction\nof its location,\nits mounting\nconfiguration,\nand its mass.\nThe input\nexcitation\nspectrum\nhas been established\nusing data from previous\nflight or\nground\ntests and is provided\nin the Spacelab\nPayload\nAccomodation\nHandbook\n(ref. 2). The spectral\ndensity at the resonance\nfrequency\nof the component\nis\nfound\n\nfrom\n\nis indicative\n\nthis data.\n\nThe dynamic\n\nof the system\n\ndamping\n\nmagnification\n\nfactor\n\nand is determined\n\n92\n\nat resonance\nexperimentally.\n\nQ = 1/2 _,\nFor\n\ncomponents mounted on isolators, Q is determined from the manufacturer\'s\ndata on the isolator mounting. These values are substituted in Eqn (1) to obtain\nthe design random load factor, Nr. As the random vibration environment occurs\nsimultaneously with other load environments during various mission phases,\nthe estimated values of N, are combined with the appropriate quasi-static,\nthermal, pressure, and crew-induced load factors to generate design load cases\nfor component analysis.\nDue to the assumptions inherent in Miles\' relation and the idealization of the\ncomponent as a single-degree-of-freedom resonator, the computed random\nload factors will be approximate. They tend to be overly conservative,\nespecially when the natural frequency of the system is close to the peak\nfrequency of the excitation spectrum. When the predicted random loads are\nunreasonably high, they lead to difficult design problems and alternate\napproaches are necessary to refine the random load estimates.\nANALYSIS BASED ON SPECTRAL ANALYSIS\nThe dynamic behaviour of large structural/mechanical systems can be\nadequately predicted only by multi-degree-of-freedom (MDOF) models. For\nlinear MDOF systems, the dynamic characteristics are specified by a matrix of\ntransfer functions, [H], whose elements Hjkrepresent the ratio of steady-state\nresponse at point j to a sinusoidal excitation at point k. For displacement\nresponse\n[H(co)]= [-[M] co i[B] co+ [K]]-1\n2+\nwhere\n\n[M], [B], and [K] represent\n\nthe discrete\n\nmodel\n\nthe mass,\n\ndamping,\n\nand co is the excitation\n\n(4)\n\nand stiffness\n\nmatrices\n\nof\n\nfrequency.\n\nThe response\nof linear MDOF systems\nsubjected\nto random excitation\ncan be\ncomputed\nusing well-established\nspectral\nanalysis\ntechniques.\nAccording\nto\nthe theory of random vibrations,\nthe response\nof a linear system with transfer\nfunction\n\n[H(r.o)], subjected\n\nto a stationary\n\nrandom\n\nload\n\n{P(t)},\n\nis given\n\n[Suu(O))] [H(o))]Spp(O,))][H\'(o.))]\n=\n[\nT\nwhere\n\n[Suu (co)] and [Spp(O_)] are the matrices\n\nof response\n\nand excitation\n\nby\n\n(5)\nspectral\n\ndensity\nfunctions\nand * and T represent\nthe complex\nconjugate\nand transpose\noperations,\nrespectively.\nThese\nmatrices\nwill have real auto-spectral\ndensity\nfunctions\nas their diagonal\nelements\nand complex\ncross-spectral\ndensity\nfunctions\nas their off-diagonal\nelements.\nBy integrating\nthe area under the\nresponse\nspectral\ndensity curve, the mean square\nresponse\nat any nodal point\nin the model can be obtained.\nThe foregoing\ndevelopment\nfor mean-square\ndisplacement\nresponse\ncan be generalized\nto provide\nmean-square\nvalues\nfor\nother response\nquantities\nsuch as velocity,\nacceleration,\nor stress.\nIt is only\n\n93\n\nnecessary to replace the transfer function matrix for displacement\nby the\ncorresponding\ntransfer function matrix for the desired response.\nThe\ngeneralization\nalso applies to the excitation which may be a point force, a\nloading condition (i.e., an ensemble of applied forces that are perfectly\ncorrelated),\nor enforced motion.\nThe analysis of flight hardware subjected to random excitation can be carried\nout using the spectral analysis features of NASTRAN.\nA finite element model of\nthe component\nis created which can accurately represent all its dominant\nmodes in the excitation frequency range. The response calculations are carried\nout in two separate functional modules.\nFirst, the transfer function of the system\ncorresponding\nto the desired response is computed in the Frequency Response\nmodule and then, the power spectral densities and other response statistics are\ncomputed in the Random Analysis module. The direct or modal superposition\napproaches can be used to perform the frequency response analysis.\nFor each\nexcitation source, Pk, the nodal response, uj, is determined at a series of user\nspecified frequencies,\n_. The ratio of output to input represents the transfer\nfunction element, Hjk((Oi ). This is determined for each excitation\ntransfer function matrix, [H], is assembled from the results.\n\nsource and the\n\nIn NASTRAN, random vibration analysis is treated as a data reduction\nprocedure that is applied to the results of frequency response analysis.\nThe\ninputs to the random analysis module are the frequency responses of desired\noutput quantities due to different load sources and the auto- and cross-spectral\ndensities of these random load sources.\nEach load source is referred to by a\nseparate subcase in the case control deck and their spectral densities are\nspecified as tabular functions of frequency in bulk data cards. If the sources are\nstatistically uncorrelated,\nonly the auto-spectral\ndensities need be defined.\nThe\npower spectral densities of response are calculated using Eqn (5) and the rootmean-square\n(rms) response is evaluated by numerically integrating the area\nunder the spectral density curve. The results are printed and plotted for\nspecified degrees of freedom of the model.\nAs mentioned earlier, the random excitation applied to the structure could be a\nforce, an enforced motion, or some other general form of excitation.\nIn the case\nof Spacelab payloads, the random excitation is specified in terms of an\nacceleration\nspectrum applied at the structural support points. The "large mass"\napproach may be used to simulate this loading condition.\nThis involves\nlumping a fictitious large mass, Ma, at the degree of freedom in which the\nacceleration\nis to be enforced.\nAn applied force equal to Ma times the required\nacceleration\nis also prescribed for that degree of freedom.\nThe inertia force is\nmade so dominant through this operation that the resulting acceleration\nis very\nclose to the required value.\n\n94\n\nILLUSTRATIVE\n\nEXAMPLE\n\nThe development\nof random vibration\nload factors\nillustrated\nin this section\nusing the Neck Chamber\n\nfor flight\nPressure\n\nhardware\nSystem\n\nis\nassembly\n\n(NCPS).\nThis Life Sciences\nLaboratory\nequipment\nitem is used to study the\neffect of weightlessness\non human cardiovascular\ncontrol\nmechanisms\nand will\nbe flown on a future Space Shuttle\nmission.\nThe NCPS assembly\nis composed\nof an experiment\nenclosure\nwhich houses several\ncomponents\nincluding\na\ncentral processing\nunit, a motor control\nunit, two motor driven bellows,\nand a\npressure\ngauge (Fig. 2). The assembly\nis mounted\nin an experiment\nrack using\ntwo support\nrails which are attached\nto the front and rear rack posts on either\nside.\nThe front panel of the enclosure\nis bolted to the front rack post flanges\nat\neight locations.\nThe whole assembly\nweighs\n48 pounds,\nand the installation\nkit\nincluding\nthe slides, fittings,\nand fasteners\nweighs an additional\nfive pounds.\nA finite element\nmodel of the NCPS assembly\nis constructed\nusing mostly plate\n(CQUAD2\nand CTRIA2)\nand bar (CBAR)\nelements.\nThe model has a total of 206\ngrid points and 192 structural\nelements.The\nmasses\nof internal components\nare\nlumped at the respective\ncenters of gravity, and stiff bar elements\nare used to\nconnect\nthem to the attachment\npoints.\nThe fasteners\nare modelled\nusing rigid\nelements.\nEigenanalysis\nwas performed\non the model with free boundary\nconditions\nto verify that the model has six rigid body modes.\nThe analysis\nis\nrepeated,\nwith the rack-to-component\ninterface\npoints appropriately\nconstrained,\nin order to determine\nthe flexible\nmodes of the component.\nThe\nfirst twenty frequencies\nof the constrained\nmodel are shown in Table 1. An\ninspection\nof the modal deformation\nplots and mass participation\nfactors\nshows\nthat the first system\nmode in X direction\nis 80 Hz. The power spectral\ndensity of\nthe input excitation\ncorresponding\nto this frequency\nis found to be 0.02 g2/Hz\nfrom Figure 3. For a conservative\nestimate\nof Q = 10 for the dynamic\nmagnification\nfactor,\nMiles\' relation (Eqn 1) yields\nNrx = 15.04\nRandom\n\nload\n\nfactors,\n\ng units\n\nN,y and Nrz, are computed\n\nin a similar\n\n(6)\nmanner.\n\nRandom\nload factors can also be determined\nthrough\nspectral\nanalysis.\nThe\ncomputation\nof Nrx is described\nhere for comparison\nwith the Miles\' approach.\nThe transfer\nfunction\nof the system is first determined\nby applying\na unit\nsinusoidal\nload in the X direction\nat each point where the NCPS interfaces\nwith\nthe rack structure.\nThe load is applied through the DLOAD and RLOAD\ncards\nwhich in turn refers to DELAY,\nDPHASE,\nDAREA,\nand TABLED\ncards.\nFor a\nconstant\nphase, unit sinusoidal\ninput, the DELAY and DPHASE\ncards may be\nomitted and a unit value specified\nfor all frequencies\non the TABLED\ncard.\nThe\ninput acceleration\nspectrum\n(Fig. 3) is specified\nthrough\nRANDPS\nand TABRND\ncards.\nThis random acceleration\nis enforced\nat all the interface\npoints in the X\ndirection\nusing the large mass approach.\nA fictitious\nmass, M,, about 1000\ntimes larger than the existing grid point mass, is lumped at the interface\nX\ndegree\nof freedom\nusing a CMASS2\ncard and an equal value is specified\nin the\n\n95\n\ncorresponding DAREA load card. This produces the desired acceleration which\ncan be verified by plotting the acceleration spectrum at the input points and\ncomparing it with Figure 3. If the agreement is not adequate, the value of Ma is\nincreased until a good match is obtained.\nThe direct and modal solution techniques are used to carry out the response\ncalculations. The relative efficiency of the two approaches depends on several\nfactors including the number of modes retained in the analysis and the number\nof response frequencies. The selection of these parameters always represents\na compromise between accuracy and efficiency. The frequencies chosen for\nresponse computations should have good resolution in the vicinity of system\nresonances in order to obtain reliable estimates of rms response. A total of 150\nfrequencies in the 0 to 500 Hz range are used for this analysis. The\nspecification of damping properties in the direct and modal formulations are\nsomewhat different. In the direct formulation, structural damping proportional to\nthe stiffness matrix terms is specified both on the material data cards and as an\noverall uniform damping factor on the PARAM G data card. In the modal\nformulation, the damping factor is specified as a tabular function of frequency\nthrough the SDAMPING and TABDMP1 cards to represent the variation of\nstructural damping for different modes. A damping factor of 0.1 is used for this\nanalysis in order to be consistent with Q = 10 used in Miles\' relation.\nThe set of output points at which the response\npower spectrum\nand rms values\nare to be recovered\nmust be chosen judiciously.\nThe selection\ncan be based on\nthe same criteria\nused for choosing\nan ASET (analysis\nset of dynamic\ndegrees\nof freedom);\nnamely,\nthe points should be uniformly\ndispersed\nthroughout\nthe\nstructure\nand should include all large mass items.\nA set of 12 output points\nwere chosen\nfor the NCPS and the rms acceleration\nresponse\nat these points is\ncomputed\n(Table 2). When these values are averaged\nand the 3-sigma\nprobability\nof occurrence\ncriteria is applied,\none obtains\nNrx =\nThis\n\nis almost\n\n30%\n\nless than\n\n10.67\n\nthe value\n\ng units\n\npredicted\n\n(7)\nusing\n\nMiles\'\n\nrelation.\n\nThe rms response\ncalculated\nusing the direct and modal solution\ntechniques\nis\nsummarized\nin Table 2. For the same number\nof response\nfrequencies,\nthe\nmodal solution\nrequired\n350 seconds\nof CPU time with 20 modes included,\n450\nseconds\nwith 40 modes, 600 seconds\nwith 60 modes, whereas\nthe direct\nsolution\nrequired\n13160 seconds.\npoints on the model is shown in\n20 modes is clearly inadequate\nthe direct approach\nare virtually\ncharacteristically\npeak at 80 Hz\nvibration.\nThe effects of higher\n\nThe response\nspectrum\nat selected\noutput\nfigures 4, 5, and 6. The solution\nobtained\nwith\nwhile the plots obtained\nusing 60 modes and\nindistinguishable.\nThe spectra\nwhich corresponds\nto the first X mode of\nmodes can also be seen in the spectral\nplots.\n\n96\n\nCONCLUDING\n\nREMARKS\n\nAn alternate method of estimating random vibration load factors for design and\nanalysis of Spacelab payloads is presented.\nThis method, based on spectral\nanalysis, yields more refined random load estimates at the expense of being\ncomputationally\nmore intensive than the Miles\' approach.\nThe computational\neffort can be reduced by using the modal formulation rather than the direct\nformulation for analysis.Significant\nreductions can be obtained in the random\nload estimates using this method. While the actual reduction depends upon the\npayload configuration\nbeing analyzed, reductions of 20 to 30% are typical. This\nmethod could be used to resolve difficult design problems owing to\nunreasonably\nhigh random load predictions by the Miles\' relation.\n\n97\n\nREFERENCES\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n,\n\nHarris, C. =\n\no\n\n,\n\n118\n\n_<\no\n\nNote that when a is less than or equal to zero (ie. the target and the impactor\nare out of\ncontact)\nthen the force is also zero.\nTwo NOLIN3\ncards are used, one to with\nincreasing\nimpact force and eventually\nto unload the target as the impactor\nbegins to travel\nin the opposite\ndirection,\naway from the target.\n\nRESULTS\nOne Dimensional\n\nRod\n\nThe first problem\nanalyzed\nis the longitudinal\nimpact\nof a steel ball on a long\naluminum\nrod of constant\ncross section.\nGeometry\nand material\nproperties\nof the impactor\nand target\nare given in figure 1. The problem\nwas modeled\nusing 144 1-D rod elements\nwith each grid point having a single longitudinal\ndegree of freedom.\nTwo more degrees of\nfreedom\nwere used to model the impact\ndynamics,\nresulting\nin a total of 147 degrees of\nfreedom.\nA single lumped mass with an initial velocity\nwas used to represent\nthe impactor.\nThe Hertzian\nforce-displacement\nrelationship\nin equation\n(1) was prescribed\nusing the\nNASTRAN\nNOLIN3\ncard, as shown in the example\ninput deck in the appendix.\nThe\ncalculated\nimpact\nforce\nhistory\ncompares\nwell\nwith\nexperimentally\ndetermined\nvalues\n[4], as shown in figure 2. The calculated\nstrain response\nat the midpoint\nof the target\nbar is compared\nwith measured\nvalues in figure 3. The sign reversal\nof the\nsecond\npulse\nis caused\nby the reflected\ntensile\nstress\nwave\ngenerated\nby the incident\ncompressive\nwave reaching\nthe free end of the bar [5].\nSome insight\ninto the\nfailure\ncan be gained by tracking\nas shown in figure 4. The energy\n\ntiming\nand the location\nof the impact-induced\nthe distribution\nof energy\nin the impactor\nand\nbalance\ncan be expressed\nas:\n\nUt\n\n=\n\nKEi\n\n+ SEi + KEt\n\n+ SEt\n\nstructural\nthe target,\n\n(13)\n\nwhere\n=\n\ntotal\n\nKEi\n\n=\n\nimpactor\n\nkinetic\n\nSEi\n\n=\n\nimpactor\n\nstrain\n\nKEt\n\n=\n\ntarget\n\nUt\n\nenergy\n\nkinetic\n\nin system\n\n2\n= 1/2 mv i\n\nenergy\nenergy\n\n= fF(a)\n\n(14)\n\n(15)\n(16)\n\nda = 2/5\n\nenergy\n\nn-I\n\n(n = number\n\nof elements)\n\n1=\n\nSEt\n\n=\n\nstrain\nn-I\n\nenergy\n\n(17)\n\nof target\n2\n\n(n = number\n1=\n\n119\n\nof elements)\n\nThe total energy\nin the system,\nUt, is divided\nbetween\nthe kinetic\nenergy and\nthe strain\nenergy of the target and impactor\nin a time--varying\nmanner.\nBecause\ndamping\neffects\nare not considered,\nthe total\nsystem\nenergy\nis constant\nand equal\nto the initial\nkinetic\nenergy\nof the impactor.\nThe strain energy of the impactor\nis non---zero only during\nthe contact\ninterval\n(0 < tC/L\n< 0.4, where t = time, L = the length\nof the bar, and C =\nthe wave speed in the bar) and peaks when the contact\nforce is greatest,\napproximately\nhalfway through the contact interval.\nThe kinetic energy\nof the impactor\ndecreases\nrapidly\nas the impactor\nslows during\ncontact\nwith the target.\nEventually,\nat tC/L\n= 0.25, the\nimpactor\nvelocity\n(and\ntherefore\nits kinetic\nenergy)\ndecreases\nto zero and the elastic\nrebound\nbegins.\nThe kinetic\nenergy\nof the impactor\nnever returns\nto its initial\nlevel\nbecause\napproximately\n80% of the energy has been transferred\nto the target\nin the form of\nstrain\nenergy\nand kinetic\nenergy.\nThe strain\nand kinetic\nenergies\nin the target\nboth\nincrease\nrapidly\nduring\nthe contact\nwith the impactor\nand remain\nconstant\nafter contact\nhas ended\n(tC/L\n> 0.4).\nBoth strain\nand kinetic\nenergies\nmaintain\nequal and constant\nvalues until the compressive\nstress wave generated\nby the impact\nreaches\nthe far end of the\nfree-free\nbar (tC/L\n= 1.0).\nA tensile stress wave is generated\nwhen the compressive\npulse\nreflects\nfrom the stress free boundary\n[5]. The superposition\nof the incident\nand reflected\npulses momentarily\nleaves the bar stress-free\nwhich causes the strain energy\nto decrease\nto\nzero.\nThe kinetic\nenergy\nsimultaneously\nincreases,\nmaintaining\na conservation\nof total\nenergy.\nThe reflection\nprocess\nis repeated\nat tC/L\n= 2.0, when the reflected\npulse returns\nto the other\nend of the bar.\nSimilar\nenergy\ndissipation\ndiagrams\nmay prove useful\nin\nanalyzing\ndynamic\nfailure of more complex\nstructures.\n\nComposite\n\nPlate\n\nThe low velocity\ntransverse\nimpact\nof a composite\nplate made from Scotchply\n1003 prepreg\n[6] is now analyzed.\nThe problem\nis depicted\nschematically\nin figure 5, and is\ndescribed\nin detail\nin references\n[7,8].\nA modified\nHertzian\ncontact\nstiffness\nhas been\nproposed\n[9] for application\nto composite\nmaterials.\nSpecifically,\nequation\n(6) is replaced\nby\nkt\n\n=\n\nE3zt\n\n(18)\n\nwhere E33 t is the transverse\nmodulus\nof the plate.\nPlate membrane\nand bending\nstiffness\nmaterial\nproperties\nwere calculated\nusing the COBSTRAN\n(Composite\nBlade\nStructural\nAnalyzer)\ncomputer\ncode [10] which calculates\nelastic\nmoduli of composite\nmaterials\nfrom\nknown constituent\nproperties\nand laminate\nply orientations.\nA uniform\nsquare\nmesh of QUAD4\nelements\nwas used to model the 15.24 cm x\n15.24 cm (6 in ,, 6 in) target\nplate.\nA mesh convergence\nstudy was performed\nto establish\nthe degree\nof mesh refinement\nnecessary\nto arrive\nat a numerically\nconverged\nsolution.\nThree different\nmeshes\nwere considered,\n25 ,, 25, 49 . 49, and 61 ,, 61 elements.\nOf these,\nthe latter\ntwo produced\nessentially\nthe same strain response\nfor a given impact\nvelocity\nand\nwere therefore\nconsidered\nto be converged\nsolutions.\nThe results\npresented\nhere were\ntherefore\ncalculated\nusing the 49 - 49 element\nmodel.\nFive degrees\nof freedom\n(Ux, Uy, Uz,\n0x and 0y) were used at each nodal point,\ngiving\nthe model\na total\nof 11510 degrees\nof\nfreedom.\nThe problem\nwas solved on a Cray XMP in 52 CPU minutes.\n\n120\n\nThe impactor\nused in the tests\n[7.8] was a uniform\n2.54 cm (1 in) long,\nblunt---ended\nsteel rod of radius 0.047625\ncm (3/18 in).\nIn the analysis\na contact\nradius of\n0.047625\ncm (_/l_ in) was assumed\nin the Hertzian\ncontact\nstiffness\ncalculations.\nThe\ncalculated\nimpact\nforce history\nis shown in figure 6. Although\nno direct\nmeasurement\nof\nthe impact\nforce was obtained\nexperimenta_y,\nthe contact\ntime was measured\n[8] and found\nto be 204 microseconds.\nThis is in good agreement\nwith the calculated\nresult.\nFigure\n6\nalso shows that a secondary\nimpact\noccurs during\nthe latter\nhalf of the contact\ninterval\n(t\n= 175 psec),\nprobably\ndue to the vibration\nof the target\nplate\nduring\ncontact\nwith the\nprojectile.\nThe resulting\ndisplacement\nresponse\nof the plate is shown in figure 7, where it\nhas been assumed\nthat no damage\noccurs\nin the target\nduring\ncontact\nwith the impactor.\nThis\nassumption\nis valid\nbased\non the available\ntest data.\nUltrasonic\nC---scans\nof the\nspecimens\nafter impact\nindicate\nthat this level of impactor\nkinetic\nenergy\n(10 Joules)\nis\nvery near the threshold\nenergy\nlevel required\nto cause damage\n[8] in specimens\nof this\nlayup.\nAs a result,\nvery little damage\noccurs\nat this impactor\nvelocity.\nThe anisotropic\nbending\nstiffness\nof the\ntarge_\n(figure\n5) is evident\nfrom\nthe elliptical\ndisplacement\ncontours,\nas the flexural\ndisturbance\ntravels\nfaster in the stiffer direction\n(figure\n7).\nThe strain response\nat gage A is compared\nto the calculated\nresponse\nin figure 8.\nThe two curves\nare similar\nin amplitude\nand duration\nbut the calculated\nstrain\nappears\nto\nlag the measured\nvalues\nby approximately\n25 microseconds.\nThis may\nbe due to the\ndifficulty\nin establishing\nexperimentally\nthe precise\ntime at which contact\noccurs\nbased on\nstrain\ngage readings\ntaken\nat some distance\nfrom the point of contact.\nThe comparison\nshown in figure 9 for gage B likewise\nshows a time shift of approximately\n25 microseconds\nbetween\nthe measured\nand the calculated\nresponse.\nThe amplitude\nand duration\nof the\ncalculated\nstrain\nresponse\ncorrelate\nquite well with the measured\nsignal.\n\nSUMMARY\n\nNASTRAN\n\nA simple\nmeans\nwas demonstrated.\n\nof modehng\nA nonlinear\n\nlow\nvelocity,\nelastic contact\n\nnon---damaging\nimpact\nmodel was included\nin the\n\nusing\nfinite\n\nelement\nanalysis\nusing\nNASTRAN\ntransfer\nfunction\ndefinitions\nand nonlinear\nanalysis\ncapabihties.\nThe same contact\nlaw was used to define the force-indentation\nrelationship\nfor two different\nimpactor/target\ncombinations.\nResults\nin both cases showed\nthat\nthe\nimpact\nforce and resulting\ntransient\nstructural\nresponse\nof the target\ncompared\nwell with\nexperimentally\nmeasured\nvalues.\n\nACKNOWLEDGEMENT\nThe\nacknowledged.\n\nsupport\nof the Naval\nWeapons\nCenter\nat China\nMr. Andrew\nVictor was the consider\nlorsional\ninertias\nin its beam\nelement\nfornmlation.\nTherefore,\nall torsional\nresults\n(angular\ndisplacements\nand torques)\nare based on stiffness\nonly, and are essentially\nthose of a static\nproblem\nsolution.\nTo remedy\nthis,\ntorsional\nincrtias\nwere\nadded\nto the\ncoupled\nmass formulation.\nAt the point\nin NASTRAN\nwhere\nthe basic\nelement\nmass matrix is formed,\nno coordinate\nsystem.\nThe\n\ntorsional\n\nmass\n\nmoment\n\nof inertia\n\nof a beam\n\nis p I_, Jx/2,\n\nwhere\n\nthe mass density,\nL is the beam length,\nand J_ is the polar area moment\ninertia.\nIn the standard\nconsistent\nmass malrix\nfor a beam, 3 lhis wdue\n\np is\nof\nis\n\nbroken\nup into 2/3 and 1/3 colnponents;\n2/3 of the wllue is placed\nat the\ndiagonal,\nand 1/3 is placed at the coupled\ndegree of l\'reedom\n(the node on the\nother end of the beam).\nThe same fractions\nare used for the translational,\nor\naxial masses.\nIn NASTRAN,\nhowever,\nthe coupled\nmass formulation\nuses an\naverage\nof lumped\nand consistent\nformulations\nto reduce error.\nThis average\nchanges\nthe components\nto 5/6 and 1/6 of the total value.\nSince these values\nare currently\nused for the axial masses\nin NASTRAN,\nthey were also used for\nthe torsional\ninertias.\nElement\n\nForce\n\nCalculations\n\nNASTRAN\nelement\nforces\nare currently\ncalculated\nby multiplying\nelelnent\nstiffness\nmatrices\nby element\ndisplacement\nvectors.\nBoth damping\nand mass effects are ignored.\nThe damping\nin a stiffness\nelement\nis actually\nin\nthe form\nof a loss factor,\nwhich generates\na complex\nstiffness\nmatrix.\nAll\nstiffness\nterms\nare multiplied\nby 1.0 + b/.\nFor\nmost\ndynamic\nanalyses,\nneglecting\nthe i*l term is acceptable\nsince it is generally\nsmall.\nFor a power\nflow analysis\nof a highly reverberant\nstructure,\nhowever,\nignoring\nthe loss\nfactor is disastrous.\nIn a hi,.zhlv reverberant\nstructure,\nthe force and velocity at\na given point are close to 90 degrees\nout of phase.\nSince power flow is defined\nas the clot product\nof these two components,\na small change\nin the phase of\nthe force has large effects on the calculated\nelement\npowers.\n\norders\n\nNeglecting\nthe clelnent\nof magnitude\nless than\n\nmass\nthose\n\nmatrices,\nwhose\ncomponenls\nof the stiffness\nmalrices,\nhas\n\n139\n\narc several\nless The dominant\ntype of\npower is the flexural component\nin Plane 1, and is denoted\nby the solid curve.\nThis type of power field corresponds\nto the motion type shown in Diagram\n1 in\nFig. 5. Cheng\n\nDivision\nCenter\n20084\n\nABSTRACT\nNASHUA\nis a coupled\nfinite\nelement/boundary\nelement\ncapability\nbuilt around\nNASTRAN\nfor calculating\nthe low frequency\nfar-field\nacoustic\npressure\nfield radiated\nor\nscattered\nby an arbitrary,\nsubmerged,\nthree-dimensional,\nelastic structure\nsubjected\nto either\ninternal\ntime-harmonic\nmechanical\nloads or external\ntime-harmonic\nincident\nloadings.\nThis\npaper describes\nthe formulation\nand nse of NASIIUA\nfor solving such structural\nacoustics\nproblems\nwhen the structure\nis fluid-filled.\nNASTRAN\nis used to generate\nthe structural\nfinite element\nmodel and to perform\nmost of the required\nmatrix\noperations.\nBoth fluid\ndomains\nare modeled\nusing the boundary\nelement\ncapabililty\nin NASHUA,\nwhose matrix\nformulation\n(and the associated\nNASTRAN\nDMAP)\nfor evacuated\nstructures\ncan be used\nwith suitable interpretation\nof the matrix definitions.\nAfter computing\nsurface\npressures\nand\nnormal\nvelocities,\nfar-field\npressures\nare\newfluated\nusing\nan asymptotic\nform\nof the\nHehnholtz\nexterior\nintegral\nequation.\nThe proposed\nnumerical\napproach\nis validated\nby\ncomparing\nthe acoustic\ntield scattered\nfrom a submerged\nfluid-filled\nspherical\nthin shell to\nthat obtained\nwith a series solution,\nwhich is also derived\nin this paper.\n\nINTRODUCTION\nTwo basic problems\nin computational\nstructural\nacoustics\nare (1) the\nacoustic\npressure\nfield radiated\nby a general\nsubmerged\nthree-dimensional\nsubjected\nscattered\n\ncalculation\nof the\nelastic structure\n\nto internal\ntime-harmonic\nloads,\nand (2) the calculation\nof the acoustic\npressure\nby such a structure\nsubjected\nto an incident\ntime-harmonic\nwavetrain.\nThe most\n\ncommon,\nas well as the most accurate,\napproach\nfor solving\nthese\nproblems\nat low\nfrequencies\nis to couple\na linilc elcrncnt\nmodel\nof the structure\nwith a boundary\nelement\nmodel\nof the surrounding\nfluid.\nThis is the approach\ntaken\nby NASHUA,\nwhich\nis a\nboundary\nelement\nprogram\nbuilt around\nNASq\'RAN,\na widely-used\nfinite element\ncomputer\nprogram\nfor structural\ndynamics.\nSeveral previous\npapers\n(Ref. 1-4) described\nthe basic formulation\nand development\nfor\nacoustic\nradiation\nand\nscattering\nfrom\nevacuated\nstructures.\nHere\nwe describe\nthe\nformulation\nand use of NASIIUA\nfor modeling\nsubmerged\nstructures\nwhich are fluid-filled.\nInternal\nfluid can occur because\nthe structure\nis free-flooded\nor contains\nfluid-filled\ntanks.\nIt\nis possible\nto use existing\nNASTRAN\ncapability\nto model\nthe interior\nfluid with finite\nelements\n(Ref. 5-7), but three-dimensional\nmodels\nwith large nulnbers\nof fluid degrees\nof\nfreedom\nmight\nresult.\nAn attractive\naltcrnative\nto the tluid finite element\nmodel\nis to\nrepresent\n\nthe contained\n\nl]uid using\n\na boundary\n\nclement\n\n150\n\napproach.\n\nInprinciplc,\n\nany computer\n\nprogram\ncapable\nof generating\nthe appropriate\nboundary\nelement\nmatrices\nfor an exterior\nfluid is also capable\nof generating\nsuch matrices\nfor the complementary\nregion (the interior\nregion).\nNASTRAN\'s\nversatility\nin user-controlled\nmatrix\noperations\n(DMAP)\nmakes\ntile\nimplementation\n\nof such\n\nan approach\n\nstraightforward.\n\nTHEORETICAL\n\nAPPROACH\n\nThe basic theoretical\ndevelopment\nfor describes\ntile changes\nnecessary\nto\nmodel\nthe interior\nlluid with boundary\nelements\nin the same\nprocedure.\nThere\nis no\nrequirement\n\nthat the interior\n\nand\n\nexterior\n\nThe Next, NASTRAN\nDMAP\nis used\nto recover\nthe surface\nnormal\nvelocities\nvn and the vector\nv of velocities\nat all structural\nDOF (NASTRAN\'s\n"g-set").\nThis step completes\nthe surface\nsolution.\nThen, with the total\npressures\nand velocities\non the (exterior)\nsurface,\ntile asymptotic\n(far-ticld)\nform\nof the\nHelmholtz\nexterior\nintegral\nequation\nis integrated\nin program\nFAROUT\nto compute\nlhc farfield radiatcd\npressures.\nVarious\ntables and graphical\ndisplays are generated.\nThe overall setup of the solution\nprocedure\nis organized\ninto four steps.\nIn Step 1, a\nseparate\nNASTRAN\nstructural\nmodel is prepared\nand run for each unique\nset of symmetry\nconstraints\nand each fluid region.\nSince, for general\nthree-dimensional\nanalysis,\nup to three\nplanes of rellective\nsymmetry\nare allowed,\nthere would be one, two, four, or cight such runs\nfor each fluid region.\nSince the purpose\nof this step is to generate\na file containing\ngeometry\ninformation\nand a checkpoint\nfile for subsequent\nuse in the other steps,\ntile only difference\nbetween\nthe two runs associated\nwith a given symmetry\ncase is the specification\nof the\noutwardly\n\ndirected\n\nunit pressure\n\nload which\n\ndefines\n\nthe wet surface\n\nfor a given\n\nlluid\n\nregion.\n\nFor each symmetry\ncase and drive frequency,\nseveral\nprograms\nare run sequentially\nto\nform Step 2. For each fluid region,\nthe SURF program\nreads the geometry\nfile generatcd\nby\nNASTRAN\nin Step 1 and, using the l:Ielmholtz\nsurface\nand interior\nintegral\nequations,\ngenerates\nthe fluid matrices\nEl, E2, CI, and C2, the area matrices\nA 1 and A2, the structurefluid transformation\nmatrices\nG1 and G2, the incident\npressure\nvector\nPI1, and a geometry\nfile to be used later by the far-field\nintegratiou\nprogram\nFAROUT\nin Step 3. In addition,\na\npartitioning\nvector\nis generated\nto facilitate\nthe merging\nand partitioning\nof the various\nmatrices\nassociated\nwith the two fluid domains.\nThe two SURF\njobs in Step 2 are followed\nby a NASTRAN\njob which\ntakes\nthe\nstructural\nmatrices\nK, M, B, and F from Step 1 and the matrices\ngeuerated\nby tile SURF\njobs and forms the matrices\nin Eq. 11, where the detinitions\nin Eq. 14 apply.\nEq. 11 is then\nsolved for the total surface\npressure\nvector p by program\nOCSOI,V,\nwhich is a general\noutof-core\nblock solver designed\nspecifically\nfor large, full, complex,\nnonsymlnetric\nsystems\nof\nlinear,\nalgebraic\nequations.\nNASTRAN\nis then re-entercd\nin Step 2 with p so that the\nvelocities\nv and v n can be rccovered\nusing DMAP\nopcrations.\nThe surface\npressures,\nnormal\nvelocities,\nand full g-set displacements\nare then reformatted,\nsorted,\nand merged\ninto\na single file (for each symlnetry\ncase) using program\nMliRGE.\nRecall that there arc one,\ntwo, four, or eight possible\nsymmetry\ncases.\nSteps 1 and 2 arc repeated\nfor each symmetry\ncase.\nAftcr all symmetry\ncases have\nbeen completed\nand merged,\nprogram\nFAROUT\n(Step 3) combines\nthe symmetry\ncases and\nintegrates\nover thc surface.\nThc far-field\npressure\nsolution\nis obtained\nby integrating\nthe\n\n155\n\nsurface\n\npressures\n\nHelmholtz\nsuitable\n\nfor\nThe\n\nthe\n\nplots\n\nvarious\n\nplots\n\ntypes\n\nbe\n\nthe\n\nin interlaally-driven\n\nNumerical\nA\n\nNASTRAN\n\nfinite\n\nelement\n\nmodel\n\nsubmerged\n\nspherical\n\nshell.\n\nSolution\nof\n\nthe\n\nspherical\n\nshell\n\nwas\n\nprepared\n\nusing\n\n40\n\naxisymmetric\nconical\nshell elements\nspanning\nthe 180 degrees\nbetween\nthe two poles of the\nsphere.\nDue to the axisymmetry\nof the incident\npressure\nloading,\nonly the N = 0 harmonic\nwas required.\nSince all structural\npoints are in contact\nwith both interior\nand exterior\nfluids,\nthe resulting\nmodel therefore\nhad 205 independent\nstructural\ndegrees\nof freedom\n(DOF)\nand\n41 fluid DOF for each of the two fluid domains.\nSystem matrices\nfor the exterior\nfluid were\nalso augmented\nby the addition\nof four constraint\nequations\nassociated\nwith interior\nChief\npoints\nto ensure\nuniqueness\nof the integral\nrepresentation\nat the upper\nfrequencies.\nThe\nnondimensional\nfrequency\nrange 0<ka<5\nwas swept using a frequency\nincrement\nof about ka\n= 0.05 with NASHUA\nand ka = 0.005 with the series solutioll.\nSince the series solution\nis\nconverged,\n\nwe treat\n\nit as an "exact"\n\nsolution\n\nfor this problem.\n\nThe comparison\nbetween\nthe computed\nand exact solutions\nis presented\nis Figs. 3 and\n4, which\nplot the frequency\nresponse\nof the nondimensional\nscattered\npressure\npr/(poa),\nwhere p is the far-field\nscattered\npressure\nat distance\nr from the origin,\nPo is the incident\npressure,\nand a is the mean radius of the spherical\nshell.\nThese two figures show very good\nagreement\nbetween\nthe two scattering\nsolutions\nin the backward\n(0 = 0) and forward\n(0 = 180\ndegrees)\ndirections.\nIn fact, the computed\nand series solutions\nare virtually indistinguislaable\nfrom each other.\n\n161\n\n5.0\n\n!\n\ni\n\n_EXACT\n_COMPUTED\n\n2.5\n\n_"\n\ni\n\n2.0\n\nO\na.\n\n1.5\n11\xc2\xa2\n\na.\n\n1.0\n\n%.J\n\n0.5\n\no0\n0.0\n\nJ\n\n\\\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n2.0\n\n1.0\n\n3.0\n\n4..0\n\n5.0\n\nKA\n\nFig.
